<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Archaea</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Archea" redirects here. For the geologic eon, see <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archean</a>. For the spider genus, see <a href="/wiki/Archaea_(spider)" title="Archaea (spider)">Archaea (spider)</a>. For the spider family, see <a href="/wiki/Archaeidae" title="Archaeidae">Archaeidae</a>. For the prefix "archae-", see <a href="/wiki/List_of_commonly_used_taxonomic_affixes" title="List of commonly used taxonomic affixes">List of commonly used taxonomic affixes</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the journal, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaea_(journal)" title="Archaea (journal)">Archaea (journal)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A domain of single-celled prokaryotic microorganisms</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

<p><b>Archaea</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Archaea.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/En-us-Archaea.ogg" title="En-us-Archaea.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ar-<span style="font-size:90%">KEE</span>-ə</i></a> or <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ar-<span style="font-size:90%">KAY</span>-ə</i></a>) constitute a <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domain</a> of <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">single-celled</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>. These microbes (<b>archaea</b>; singular <b>archaeon</b>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes">prokaryotes</a>, meaning they have no <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a>.
Archaea were initially classified as <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, receiving the name <b>archaebacteria</b> (in the Archaebacteria kingdom), but this classification is <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">outdated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeal cells have unique properties separating them from the other two domains of life, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">Eukarya</a>. The Archaea are further divided into multiple recognized <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a>. Classification is difficult because the majority have not been <a href="/wiki/Isolation_(microbiology)" title="Isolation (microbiology)">isolated</a> in the laboratory and have only been detected by analysis of their <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a> in samples from their environment.
</p><p>Archaea and bacteria are generally similar in size and shape, although a few archaea have shapes quite unlike that of bacteria, such as the flat and square-shaped cells of <i><a href="/wiki/Haloquadratum_walsbyi" title="Haloquadratum walsbyi">Haloquadratum walsbyi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Despite this morphological similarity to bacteria, archaea possess <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> that are more closely related to those of eukaryotes, notably the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> involved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> and <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a>. Other aspects of archaeal biochemistry are unique, such as their reliance on <a href="/wiki/Ether_lipid" title="Ether lipid">ether lipids</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Archaeol" title="Archaeol">archaeols</a>. Archaea use more energy sources than eukaryotes: these range from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a>, such as sugars, to <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">metal ions</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen gas</a>. Salt-tolerant archaea (the <a href="/wiki/Haloarchaea" title="Haloarchaea">Haloarchaea</a>) use sunlight as an energy source, and other species of archaea <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">fix carbon</a>, but unlike plants and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, no known species of archaea does both. Archaea <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">reproduce asexually</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(reproduction)" title="Fragmentation (reproduction)">fragmentation</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Budding" title="Budding">budding</a>; unlike bacteria and eukaryotes, no known species forms <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>.
</p><p>Archaea were initially viewed as <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophiles</a> living in harsh environments, such as <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lakes</a>, but they have since been found in a broad range of <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>, including soils, oceans, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshland" title="Marshland">marshlands</a>. They are also part of the <a href="/wiki/Human_microbiota" title="Human microbiota">human microbiota</a>, found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colon_(anatomy)" title="Colon (anatomy)">colon</a>, mouth, and skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bang2015_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bang2015-8">[8]</a></sup> Archaea are particularly numerous in the oceans, and the archaea in <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> may be one of the most abundant groups of organisms on the planet. Archaea are a major part of Earth's life and may play roles in both the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a>. No clear examples of archaeal <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> are known, but they are often <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualists</a> or <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensals</a>. One example is the <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a> that inhabit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">human</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminant</a> guts, where their vast numbers aid <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a>. Methanogens are also used in <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> production and <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> exploits enzymes from extremophile archaea that can endure high temperatures and organic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solvents" title="Solvents">solvents</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span id="A_new_domain"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_domain">New domain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: New domain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1277" data-file-width="1999" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg/220px-Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg/330px-Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg/440px-Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaea were first found in extreme environments, such as volcanic <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a>. Pictured here is <a href="/wiki/Grand_Prismatic_Spring" title="Grand Prismatic Spring">Grand Prismatic Spring</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For much of the 20th century, prokaryotes were regarded as a single group of organisms and classified based on their <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. For example, microbiologists tried to classify microorganisms based on the structures of their <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a>, their shapes, and the substances they consume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1965, <a href="/wiki/Emile_Zuckerkandl" title="Emile Zuckerkandl">Emile Zuckerkandl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> proposed instead using the sequences of the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> in different prokaryotes to work out how they are related to each other. This <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> approach is the main method used today.
</p><p>Archaea were first classified as a separate group of prokaryotes in 1977 by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">Carl Woese</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_E._Fox" title="George E. Fox">George E. Fox</a> based on the sequences of <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> (rRNA) genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">[11]</a></sup> These two groups were originally named the Archaebacteria and Eubacteria and were termed <i>Urkingdoms</i> by Woese and Fox; other researchers treated them as <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdoms</a> or subkingdoms. In this publication Woese and Fox presented the very first pieces of evidence for the concept of archaebacteria — which at that time contained only the methanogens — as a separate "line of descent": 1. lack of <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a> in their cell walls, 2. two unusual coenzymes, 3. results of <a href="/wiki/16S_ribosomal_RNA" title="16S ribosomal RNA">16S ribosomal RNA</a> gene sequencing. To emphasize this difference, Woese, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Kandler" title="Otto Kandler">Otto Kandler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Wheelis" title="Mark Wheelis">Mark Wheelis</a> later proposed a new natural system of organisms with three separate <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">Domains</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukarya" title="Eukarya">Eukarya</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> and the Archaea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woese-1">[1]</a></sup> in what is now known as "The Woesian Revolution". The history of the research on Archaea is documented in detail by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Sapp" title="Jan Sapp">Jan Sapp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The word <i>archaea</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B1%E1%BF%96%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ἀρχαῖος">ἀρχαῖα</a></span>, meaning "ancient things",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> as the first representatives of the domain Archaea were <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a> and it was assumed that their metabolism reflected Earth's primitive atmosphere and the organisms' antiquity, but as new habitats were studied, more organisms were discovered. Extreme <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halophilic" title="Halophilic">halophilic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magrum_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magrum-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperthermophilic" title="Hyperthermophilic">hyperthermophilic</a> microbes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stetter_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stetter-15">[15]</a></sup> were also included in the Archaea. For a long time, archaea were seen as extremophiles that only exist in extreme habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lakes</a>. By the end of the 20th century, archaea had been identified in non-extreme environments as well. Today, they are known to be a large and diverse group of organisms that are widely distributed in nature and are common in all habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeLong_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeLong-16">[16]</a></sup> This new appreciation of the importance and ubiquity of archaea came from using <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR) to detect prokaryotes from environmental samples (such as water or soil) by multiplying their ribosomal genes. This allows the detection and identification of organisms that have not been <a href="/wiki/Microbiological_culture" title="Microbiological culture">cultured</a> in the laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_classification">Current classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Current classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">Biological classification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">Systematics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="650" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg/220px-Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg/330px-Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg/440px-Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Archaeal_Richmond_Mine_acidophilic_nanoorganisms" title="Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms">ARMAN</a> are a new group of archaea recently discovered in <a href="/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage" title="Acid mine drainage">acid mine drainage</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The classification of archaea, and of prokaryotes in general, is a rapidly moving and contentious field. Current classification systems aim to organize archaea into groups of organisms that share structural features and common ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gevers_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gevers-19">[19]</a></sup> These classifications rely heavily on the use of the sequence of <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> genes to reveal relationships between organisms (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogeny" title="Molecular phylogeny">molecular phylogenetics</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-20">[20]</a></sup> Most of the culturable and well-investigated species of archaea are members of two main <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Euryarchaeota" title="Euryarchaeota">Euryarchaeota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crenarchaeota" title="Crenarchaeota">Crenarchaeota</a>. Other groups have been tentatively created. For example, the peculiar species <i><a href="/wiki/Nanoarchaeum_equitans" title="Nanoarchaeum equitans">Nanoarchaeum equitans</a></i>, which was discovered in 2003, has been given its own phylum, the <a href="/wiki/Nanoarchaeota" title="Nanoarchaeota">Nanoarchaeota</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A new phylum <a href="/wiki/Korarchaeota" title="Korarchaeota">Korarchaeota</a> has also been proposed. It contains a small group of unusual thermophilic species that shares features of both of the main phyla, but is most closely related to the Crenarchaeota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Other recently detected species of archaea are only distantly related to any of these groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Archaeal_Richmond_Mine_acidophilic_nanoorganisms" title="Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms">Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms</a> (ARMAN, comprising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrarchaeota" title="Micrarchaeota">Micrarchaeota</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parvarchaeota" title="Parvarchaeota">Parvarchaeota</a>), which were discovered in 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and are some of the smallest organisms known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>A superphylum – TACK – has been proposed that includes the <a href="/wiki/Thaumarchaeota" title="Thaumarchaeota">Thaumarchaeota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aigarchaeota" title="Aigarchaeota">Aigarchaeota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crenarchaeota" title="Crenarchaeota">Crenarchaeota</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Korarchaeota" title="Korarchaeota">Korarchaeota</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guy2011_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guy2011-26">[26]</a></sup> This superphylum may be related to the origin of eukaryotes. More recently, the superphylum <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(archaea)" title="Asgard (archaea)">Asgard</a> has been named and proposed to be more closely related to the original eukaryote and a sister group to TACK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZN_Asgard2017_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZN_Asgard2017-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_of_species">Concept of species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Concept of species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The classification of archaea into species is also controversial. Biology defines a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> as a group of related organisms. The familiar exclusive breeding criterion (organisms that can breed with each other <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_isolation" title="Reproductive isolation">but not with others</a>) is of no help here because archaea reproduce asexually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaea show high levels of <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a> between lineages. Some researchers suggest that individuals can be grouped into species-like populations given highly similar genomes and infrequent gene transfer to/from cells with less-related genomes, as in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ferroplasma" title="Ferroplasma">Ferroplasma</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> On the other hand, studies in <i><a href="/wiki/Halorubrum" title="Halorubrum">Halorubrum</a></i> found significant genetic transfer to/from less-related populations, limiting the criterion's applicability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> A second concern is to what extent such species designations have practical meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Current knowledge on genetic diversity is fragmentary and the total number of archaeal species cannot be estimated with any accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-20">[20]</a></sup> Estimates of the number of phyla range from 18 to 23, of which only 8 have representatives that have been cultured and studied directly. Many of these hypothesized groups are known from a single rRNA sequence, indicating that the diversity among these organisms remains obscure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Bacteria also contain many uncultured microbes with similar implications for characterization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>On average, archaeal DNA sequences (whole genome) show higher levels of complexity than those of Bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratas_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pratas-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">Origin and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_evolution" title="Timeline of evolution">Timeline of evolution</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a> is about 4.54 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS1997_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1997-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Scientific evidence suggests that <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">life began</a> on Earth at least 3.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billion_years_ago" title="Billion years ago">billion years ago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmSci_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmSci-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AT-20120904_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-AT-20120904-39">[39]</a></sup> The earliest evidence for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a> is <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> found to be <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic</a> in 3.7 billion-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">metasedimentary rocks</a> discovered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-40">[40]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> discovered in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-42">[42]</a></sup> More recently, in 2015, "remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic life</a>" were found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although probable prokaryotic cell <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> date to almost 3.5 <a href="/wiki/Bya" title="Bya">billion years ago</a>, most prokaryotes do not have distinctive morphologies and fossil shapes cannot be used to identify them as archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Instead, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_fossil" title="Chemical fossil">chemical fossils</a> of unique <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> are more informative because such compounds do not occur in other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Some publications suggest that archaeal or eukaryotic lipid remains are present in <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shales</a> dating from 2.7 billion years ago;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> such data have since been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Such lipids have also been detected in even older rocks from west <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>. The oldest such traces come from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isua_greenstone_belt" title="Isua greenstone belt">Isua district</a>, which includes Earth's oldest known sediments, formed 3.8 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The archaeal lineage may be the most ancient that exists on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Woese argued that the bacteria, archaea, and eukaryotes represent separate lines of descent that diverged early on from an ancestral colony of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woese1-52">[52]</a></sup> One possibility<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese1_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woese1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandler_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kandler-53">[53]</a></sup> is that this occurred before the <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cells" title="Evolution of cells">evolution of cells</a>, when the lack of a typical cell membrane allowed unrestricted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateral_gene_transfer" title="Lateral gene transfer">lateral gene transfer</a>, and that the common ancestors of the three domains arose by fixation of specific subsets of genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese1_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Woese1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandler_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kandler-53">[53]</a></sup> It is possible that the last common ancestor of the bacteria and archaea was a <a href="/wiki/Thermophile" title="Thermophile">thermophile</a>, which raises the possibility that lower temperatures are "extreme environments" in archaeal terms, and organisms that live in cooler environments appeared only later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Since the Archaea and Bacteria are no more related to each other than they are to eukaryotes, the term <i>prokaryote'</i>s only surviving meaning is "not a eukaryote", limiting its value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID8177167_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID8177167-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_other_domains">Comparison to other domains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Comparison to other domains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following table compares some major characteristics of the three domains, to illustrate their similarities and differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Many of these characteristics are also discussed below.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Property
</th>
<th>Archaea

<th>Bacteria
</th>
<th>Eukarya
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">Cell membrane</a></b>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">Ether</a>-linked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudopeptidoglycan" title="Pseudopeptidoglycan">pseudopeptidoglycan</a>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">Ester</a>-linked lipids, <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td>
<td>Photosynthesis, cellular respiration and fermentation
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">Reproduction</a></b>
</td>

</td>
<td>Asexual reproduction, horizontal gene transfer
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual</a> and asexual reproduction
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Archaea were split off as a third domain because of the large differences in their ribosomal RNA structure. The particular RNA molecule sequenced, known as <a href="/wiki/16S_rRNA" title="16S rRNA">16S rRNA</a>, is present in all organisms and always has the same vital function: the production of proteins. Because this function is so central to life, organisms with mutations of their 16S rRNA are unlikely to survive, leading to great stability in the structure of this nucleotide over many generations. 16S rRNA is also large enough to retain organism-specific information, but small enough to be sequenced in a manageable amount of time. In 1977, Carl Woese, a microbiologist studying the genetic sequencing of organisms, developed a new sequencing method that involved splitting the RNA into fragments that could be sorted and compared to other fragments from other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">[11]</a></sup> The more similar the patterns between species were, the more closely related the organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Woese used his new rRNA comparison method to categorize and contrast different organisms. He sequenced a variety of different species and happened upon a group of methanogens that had vastly different patterns than any known prokaryotes or eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">[11]</a></sup> These methanogens were much more similar to each other than they were to other organisms sequenced, leading Woese to propose the new domain of Archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">[11]</a></sup> His experiments showed that the Archaea were more similar to eukaryotes than prokaryotes, even though they were more similar to prokaryotes in structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-60">[60]</a></sup> This led to the conclusion that Archaea and Eukarya shared a more recent common ancestor than Eukarya and Bacteria in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-60">[60]</a></sup> The development of the nucleus occurred after the split between Bacteria and this common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-60">[60]</a></sup> Although Archaea are prokaryotic, they are more closely related to Eukarya and thus cannot be placed within either the Bacteria or Eukarya domains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Woese-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>One property unique to Archaea is the abundant use of ether-linked lipids in their cell membranes. Ether linkages are more chemically stable than the ester linkages found in Bacteria and Eukarya, which may be a contributing factor to the ability of many Archaea to survive in extreme environments that place heavy stress on cell membranes, such as extreme heat and <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>. Comparative analysis of archaeal genomes has also identified several molecular signatures in the form of <a href="/wiki/Conserved_signature_indels" title="Conserved signature indels">conserved signature indels</a> and signature proteins which are uniquely present in either all Archaea or different main groups within Archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Another unique feature of Archaea is that no other known organisms are capable of <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">methanogenesis</a> (the metabolic production of methane). Methanogenic Archaea play a pivotal role in ecosystems with organisms that derive energy from oxidation of methane, many of which are Bacteria, as they are often a major source of methane in such environments and can play a role as primary producers. <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">Methanogens</a> also play a critical role in the carbon cycle, breaking down organic carbon into methane, which is also a major greenhouse gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_bacteria">Relationship to bacteria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Relationship to bacteria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_414180934"><area alt="Euryarchaeota" coords="28,165,99,175" href="/wiki/Euryarchaeota" shape="rect" title="Euryarchaeota"/><area alt="Nanoarchaeota" coords="16,145,90,153" href="/wiki/Nanoarchaeota" shape="rect" title="Nanoarchaeota"/><area alt="Crenarchaeota" coords="14,127,87,135" href="/wiki/Crenarchaeota" shape="rect" title="Crenarchaeota"/><area alt="Protozoa" coords="36,96,80,104" href="/wiki/Protozoa" shape="rect" title="Protozoa"/><area alt="Algae" coords="61,63,89,72" href="/wiki/Algae" shape="rect" title="Algae"/><area alt="Plantae" coords="70,47,100,55" href="/wiki/Plantae" shape="rect" title="Plantae"/><area alt="Slime molds" coords="51,34,118,42" href="/wiki/Slime_molds" shape="rect" title="Slime molds"/><area alt="Animal" coords="99,21,140,29" href="/wiki/Animal" shape="rect" title="Animal"/><area alt="Fungus" coords="146,19,173,28" href="/wiki/Fungus" shape="rect" title="Fungus"/><area alt="Gram-positive bacteria" coords="179,15,253,25" href="/wiki/Gram-positive_bacteria" shape="rect" title="Gram-positive bacteria"/><area alt="Chlamydiae" coords="192,28,249,38" href="/wiki/Chlamydiae" shape="rect" title="Chlamydiae"/><area alt="Chloroflexi" coords="211,42,333,50" href="/wiki/Chloroflexi_(phylum)" shape="rect" title="Chloroflexi"/><area alt="Actinobacteria" coords="235,58,307,66" href="/wiki/Actinobacteria" shape="rect" title="Actinobacteria"/><area alt="Planctomycetes" coords="250,74,328,84" href="/wiki/Planctomycetes" shape="rect" title="Planctomycetes"/><area alt="Spirochaetes" coords="255,93,319,103" href="/wiki/Spirochaetes" shape="rect" title="Spirochaetes"/><area alt="Fusobacteria" coords="255,118,318,125" href="/wiki/Fusobacteria" shape="rect" title="Fusobacteria"/><area alt="Cyanobacteria" coords="254,137,343,160" href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" shape="rect" title="Cyanobacteria"/><area alt="Thermophiles" coords="240,164,321,184" href="/wiki/Thermophiles" shape="rect" title="Thermophiles"/><area alt="Acidobacteria" coords="212,194,281,202" href="/wiki/Acidobacteria" shape="rect" title="Acidobacteria"/><area alt="Proteobacteria" coords="129,205,201,213" href="/wiki/Proteobacteria" shape="rect" title="Proteobacteria"/></map><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="1122" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/350px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/700px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_414180934" width="350"/></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing the relationship between the Archaea and other domains of life. <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">Eukaryotes</a> are colored red, archaea green and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> blue. Adapted from Ciccarelli et al. (2006)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cicc06_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cicc06-65">[65]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The relationship between the three domains is of central importance for understanding the origin of life. Most of the metabolic pathways, which are the object of the majority of an organism's genes, are common between Archaea and Bacteria, while most genes involved in genome expression are common between Archaea and Eukarya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Within prokaryotes, archaeal cell structure is most similar to that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram-positive" title="Gram-positive">gram-positive</a> bacteria, largely because both have a single lipid bilayer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-1_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-1-67">[67]</a></sup> and usually contain a thick sacculus (exoskeleton) of varying chemical composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In some phylogenetic trees based upon different gene/protein sequences of prokaryotic homologs, the archaeal homologs are more closely related to those of gram-positive bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-1_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-1-67">[67]</a></sup> Archaea and gram-positive bacteria also share conserved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indels" title="Indels">indels</a> in a number of important proteins, such as <a href="/wiki/Hsp70" title="Hsp70">Hsp70</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutamine_synthetase" title="Glutamine synthetase">glutamine synthetase</a> I;,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-1_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-1-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-2-69">[69]</a></sup> but the phylogeny of these genes was interpreted to reveal interdomain gene transfer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and might not reflect the organismal relationship(s).
</p><p>It has been proposed that the archaea evolved from gram-positive bacteria in response to antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">selection pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-1_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-1-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-2_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-2-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-3_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-3-72">[72]</a></sup> This is suggested by the observation that archaea are resistant to a wide variety of antibiotics that are primarily produced by gram-positive bacteria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-1_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-1-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-2_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-2-69">[69]</a></sup> and that these antibiotics primarily act on the genes that distinguish archaea from bacteria. The proposal is that the selective pressure towards resistance generated by the gram-positive antibiotics was eventually sufficient to cause extensive changes in many of the antibiotics' target genes, and that these strains represented the common ancestors of present-day Archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-3_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-3-72">[72]</a></sup> The evolution of Archaea in response to antibiotic selection, or any other competitive selective pressure, could also explain their adaptation to extreme environments (such as high temperature or acidity) as the result of a search for unoccupied niches to escape from antibiotic-producing organisms;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta-3_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-3-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith" title="Thomas Cavalier-Smith">Cavalier-Smith</a> has made a similar suggestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> This proposal is also supported by other work investigating protein structural relationships<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and studies that suggest that gram-positive bacteria may constitute the earliest branching lineages within the prokaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_eukaryotes">Relation to eukaryotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Relation to eukaryotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The evolutionary relationship between archaea and <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> remains unclear. Aside from the similarities in cell structure and function that are discussed below, many genetic trees group the two.
</p><p>Complicating factors include claims that the relationship between eukaryotes and the archaeal phylum <a href="/wiki/Crenarchaeota" title="Crenarchaeota">Crenarchaeota</a> is closer than the relationship between the <a href="/wiki/Euryarchaeota" title="Euryarchaeota">Euryarchaeota</a> and the phylum Crenarchaeota<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-77">[77]</a></sup> and the presence of archaea-like genes in certain bacteria, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Thermotogae" title="Thermotogae">Thermotoga maritima</a></i>, from <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The standard hypothesis states that the ancestor of the eukaryotes diverged early from the Archaea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and that eukaryotes arose through fusion of an archaean and eubacterium, which became the nucleus and <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a>; this explains various genetic similarities but runs into difficulties explaining cell structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-77">[77]</a></sup> An alternative hypothesis, the <a href="/wiki/Eocyte_hypothesis" title="Eocyte hypothesis">eocyte hypothesis</a>, posits that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a> emerged relatively late from the Archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2domains_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-2domains-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>A lineage of archaea discovered in 2015, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lokiarchaeum" title="Lokiarchaeum">Lokiarchaeum</a></i> (of proposed new Phylum "<a href="/wiki/Lokiarchaeota" title="Lokiarchaeota">Lokiarchaeota</a>"), named for a <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a> called <a href="/wiki/Loki%27s_Castle" title="Loki's Castle">Loki's Castle</a> in the Arctic Ocean, was found to be the most closely related to eukaryotes known at that time. It has been called a transitional organism between prokaryotes and eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150506_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150506-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several sister phyla of "Lokiarchaeota" have since been found ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorarchaeota" title="Thorarchaeota">Thorarchaeota</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odinarchaeota" title="Odinarchaeota">Odinarchaeota</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heimdallarchaeota" title="Heimdallarchaeota">Heimdallarchaeota</a>"), all together comprising a newly proposed supergroup <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(archaea)" title="Asgard (archaea)">Asgard</a>, which may appear as a sister taxon to TACK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZN_Asgard2017_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZN_Asgard2017-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_taxonomy_page_on_Archaea_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI_taxonomy_page_on_Archaea-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Details of the relation of Asgard members and eukaryotes are still under consideration.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Individual archaea range from 0.1 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometers</a> (μm) to over 15 μm in diameter, and occur in various shapes, commonly as spheres, rods, spirals or plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergey_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergey-85">[85]</a></sup> Other morphologies in the <a href="/wiki/Crenarchaeota" title="Crenarchaeota">Crenarchaeota</a> include irregularly shaped lobed cells in <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus" title="Sulfolobus">Sulfolobus</a></i>, needle-like filaments that are less than half a micrometer in diameter in <i><a href="/wiki/Thermofilum" title="Thermofilum">Thermofilum</a></i>, and almost perfectly rectangular rods in <i><a href="/wiki/Thermoproteus" title="Thermoproteus">Thermoproteus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrobaculum" title="Pyrobaculum">Pyrobaculum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Archaea in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Haloquadratum" title="Haloquadratum">Haloquadratum</a></i> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Haloquadratum_walsbyi" title="Haloquadratum walsbyi">Haloquadratum walsbyi</a></i> are flat, square archaea that live in hypersaline pools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsby1980_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walsby1980-87">[87]</a></sup> These unusual shapes are probably maintained both by their cell walls and a <a href="/wiki/Prokaryotic_cytoskeleton" title="Prokaryotic cytoskeleton">prokaryotic cytoskeleton</a>. Proteins related to the cytoskeleton components of other organisms exist in archaea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and filaments form within their cells,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> but in contrast to other organisms, these cellular structures are poorly understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Thermoplasma" title="Thermoplasma">Thermoplasma</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ferroplasma" title="Ferroplasma">Ferroplasma</a></i> the lack of a <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a> means that the cells have irregular shapes, and can resemble <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amoeboid" title="Amoeboid">amoebae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golyshina_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golyshina-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some species form aggregates or filaments of cells up to 200 μm long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergey_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bergey-85">[85]</a></sup> These organisms can be prominent in <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Notably, aggregates of <i><a href="/wiki/Thermococcus" title="Thermococcus">Thermococcus</a> coalescens</i> cells fuse together in culture, forming single giant cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Archaea in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrodictium" title="Pyrodictium">Pyrodictium</a></i> produce an elaborate multicell colony involving arrays of long, thin hollow tubes called <i>cannulae</i> that stick out from the cells' surfaces and connect them into a dense bush-like agglomeration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The function of these cannulae is not settled, but they may allow communication or nutrient exchange with neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Multi-species colonies exist, such as the "string-of-pearls" community that was discovered in 2001 in a German swamp. Round whitish colonies of a novel Euryarchaeota species are spaced along thin filaments that can range up to 15 centimetres (5.9 in) long; these filaments are made of a particular bacteria species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Structure.2C_composition_development.2C_and_operation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure,_composition_development,_and_operation">Structure, composition development, and operation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Structure, composition development, and operation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Archaea and bacteria have generally similar <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell</a> structure, but cell composition and organization set the archaea apart. Like bacteria, archaea lack interior membranes and <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID8177167_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-PMID8177167-55">[55]</a></sup> Like bacteria, the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a> of archaea are usually bounded by a <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a> and they swim using one or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagella" title="Flagella">flagella</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-97">[97]</a></sup> Structurally, archaea are most similar to <a href="/wiki/Gram-positive_bacteria" title="Gram-positive bacteria">gram-positive bacteria</a>. Most have a single plasma membrane and cell wall, and lack a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periplasmic_space" title="Periplasmic space">periplasmic space</a>; the exception to this general rule is <i><a href="/wiki/Ignicoccus" title="Ignicoccus">Ignicoccus</a></i>, which possess a particularly large periplasm that contains membrane-bound <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology)" title="Vesicle (biology)">vesicles</a> and is enclosed by an outer membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_wall_and_flagella">Cell wall and flagella</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cell wall and flagella">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall#Archaeal_cell_walls" title="Cell wall">Cell wall § Archaeal cell walls</a></div>
<p>Most archaea (but not <i><a href="/wiki/Thermoplasma" title="Thermoplasma">Thermoplasma</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ferroplasma" title="Ferroplasma">Ferroplasma</a></i>) possess a cell wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golyshina_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Golyshina-91">[91]</a></sup> In most archaea the wall is assembled from surface-layer proteins, which form an <a href="/wiki/S-layer" title="S-layer">S-layer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> An S-layer is a rigid array of protein molecules that cover the outside of the cell (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain_mail" title="Chain mail">chain mail</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> This layer provides both chemical and physical protection, and can prevent <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> from contacting the cell membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandler1998_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kandler1998-101">[101]</a></sup> Unlike bacteria, archaea lack <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a> in their cell walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howland_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howland-102">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methanobacteriales" title="Methanobacteriales">Methanobacteriales</a> do have cell walls containing <a href="/wiki/Pseudopeptidoglycan" title="Pseudopeptidoglycan">pseudopeptidoglycan</a>, which resembles eubacterial peptidoglycan in morphology, function, and physical structure, but pseudopeptidoglycan is distinct in chemical structure; it lacks <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-amino_acids" title="D-amino acids">D-amino acids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-acetylmuramic_acid" title="N-acetylmuramic acid">N-acetylmuramic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandler1998_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kandler1998-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaea <a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">flagella</a> operate like bacterial flagella—their long stalks are driven by rotatory motors at the base. These motors are powered by the <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">proton gradient</a> across the membrane, but archaeal flagella are notably different in composition and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-97">[97]</a></sup> The two types of flagella evolved from different ancestors. The bacterial flagellum shares a common ancestor with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_III_secretion_system" title="Type III secretion system">type III secretion system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> while archaeal flagella appear to have evolved from bacterial type IV <a href="/wiki/Pilus" title="Pilus">pili</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In contrast to the bacterial flagellum, which is hollow and is assembled by subunits moving up the central pore to the tip of the flagella, archaeal flagella are synthesized by adding subunits at the base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Membranes">Membranes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Membranes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archaea_membrane.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="324" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Archaea_membrane.svg/300px-Archaea_membrane.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Archaea_membrane.svg/450px-Archaea_membrane.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Archaea_membrane.svg/600px-Archaea_membrane.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archaea_membrane.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Membrane structures. <b>Top</b>, an archaeal phospholipid: <b>1</b>, isoprene chains; <b>2</b>, ether linkages; <b>3</b>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levorotation_and_dextrorotation" title="Levorotation and dextrorotation">L-glycerol</a> moiety; <b>4</b>, phosphate group. <b>Middle</b>, a bacterial or eukaryotic phospholipid: <b>5</b>, fatty acid chains; <b>6</b>, ester linkages; <b>7</b>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levorotation_and_dextrorotation" title="Levorotation and dextrorotation">D-glycerol</a> moiety; <b>8</b>, phosphate group. <b>Bottom</b>: <b>9</b>, lipid bilayer of bacteria and eukaryotes; <b>10</b>, lipid monolayer of some archaea.</div></div></div>
<p>Archaeal membranes are made of molecules that are distinctly different from those in all other life forms, showing that archaea are related only distantly to bacteria and eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koga_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koga-107">[107]</a></sup> In all organisms, <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a> are made of molecules known as <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>. These molecules possess both a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> part that dissolves in water (the <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> "head"), and a "greasy" non-polar part that does not (the lipid tail). These dissimilar parts are connected by a <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> moiety. In water, phospholipids cluster, with the heads facing the water and the tails facing away from it. The major structure in cell membranes is a double layer of these phospholipids, which is called a <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid bilayer</a>.
</p><p>The phospholipids of archaea are unusual in four ways:
</p>
<ul><li>They have membranes composed of glycerol-<a href="/wiki/Ether_lipid" title="Ether lipid">ether lipids</a>, whereas bacteria and eukaryotes have membranes composed mainly of glycerol-<a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The difference is the type of bond that joins the lipids to the glycerol moiety; the two types are shown in yellow in the figure at the right. In ester lipids this is an <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester bond</a>, whereas in ether lipids this is an <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether bond</a>. Ether bonds are chemically more resistant than ester bonds.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Stereochemistry" title="Stereochemistry">stereochemistry</a> of the archaeal glycerol moiety is the mirror image of that found in other organisms. The glycerol moiety can occur in two forms that are mirror images of one another, called <i><a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomers</a></i>. Just as a right hand does not fit easily into a left-handed glove, enantiomers of one type generally cannot be used or made by <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> adapted for the other. The archaeal phospholipids are built on a backbone of <i>sn</i>-glycerol-1-phosphate, which is an enantiomer of <i>sn</i>-glycerol-3-phosphate, the phospholipid backbone found in bacteria and eucaryotes. This suggests that archaea use entirely different enzymes for synthesizing phospholipids than do bacteria and eukaryotes. Such enzymes developed very early in life's history, indicating an early split from the other two domains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koga_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koga-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>Archaeal lipid tails differ from those of other organisms in that they are based upon long <a href="/wiki/Isoprene" title="Isoprene">isoprenoid</a> chains with multiple side-branches, sometimes with <a href="/wiki/Cyclopropane" title="Cyclopropane">cyclopropane</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexane" title="Cyclohexane">cyclohexane</a> rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> By contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> in the membranes of other organisms have straight chains without side branches or rings. Although isoprenoids play an important role in the biochemistry of many organisms, only the archaea use them to make phospholipids. These branched chains may help prevent archaeal membranes from leaking at high temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li>In some archaea, the lipid bilayer is replaced by a monolayer. In effect, the archaea fuse the tails of two phospholipid molecules into a single molecule with two polar heads (a <a href="/wiki/Bolaamphiphile" title="Bolaamphiphile">bolaamphiphile</a>); this fusion may make their membranes more rigid and better able to resist harsh environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> For example, the lipids in <i><a href="/wiki/Ferroplasma" title="Ferroplasma">Ferroplasma</a></i> are of this type, which is thought to aid this organism's survival in its highly acidic habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Microbial_metabolism" title="Microbial metabolism">Microbial metabolism</a></div>
<p>Archaea exhibit a great variety of chemical reactions in their <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> and use many sources of energy. These reactions are classified into <a href="/wiki/Primary_nutritional_groups" title="Primary nutritional groups">nutritional groups</a>, depending on energy and carbon sources. Some archaea obtain energy from <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (they are <a href="/wiki/Lithotroph" title="Lithotroph">lithotrophs</a>). These include <a href="/wiki/Nitrifying_bacteria" title="Nitrifying bacteria">nitrifiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidiser" title="Oxidiser">oxidisers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valentine_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-valentine-113">[113]</a></sup> In these reactions one compound passes electrons to another (in a <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction), releasing energy to fuel the cell's activities. One compound acts as an <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">electron donor</a> and one as an <a href="/wiki/Electron_acceptor" title="Electron acceptor">electron acceptor</a>. The energy released is used to generate <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP) through <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">chemiosmosis</a>, the same basic process that happens in the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrion</a> of eukaryotic cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other groups of archaea use sunlight as a source of energy (they are <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">phototrophs</a>), but oxygen–generating <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> does not occur in any of these organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer-114">[114]</a></sup> Many basic <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> are shared between all forms of life; for example, archaea use a modified form of <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Entner%E2%80%93Doudoroff_pathway" title="Entner–Doudoroff pathway">Entner–Doudoroff pathway</a>) and either a complete or partial <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zillig_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zillig-115">[115]</a></sup> These similarities to other organisms probably reflect both early origins in the history of life and their high level of efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>

<tr>
